<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0946677e117047f4ab9ecbe3a80ed324"><span data-offset-key="0946677e117047f4ab9ecbe3a80ed324:0">**26.**</span><span data-offset-key="0946677e117047f4ab9ecbe3a80ed324:1"> Write a Python program to compute the summation of the absolute difference of all distinct pairs in a given array (non-decreasing order). </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/#EDITOR" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="5ad8fae63ddf4f1cb93f426e1837b54e"><span data-offset-key="5ad8fae63ddf4f1cb93f426e1837b54e:0">Go to the editor</span></span></a><span data-key="ad51dee510c84c7ca8901ecd4e1b16a2"><span data-offset-key="ad51dee510c84c7ca8901ecd4e1b16a2:0"> Sample array: \[1, 2, 3\] Then all the distinct pairs will be: 1 2 1 3 2 3 </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/python-basic-1-exercise-26.php" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="8ad16dc5a37949749a02258fcf16b1a1"><span data-offset-key="8ad16dc5a37949749a02258fcf16b1a1:0">Click me to see the sample solution</span></span></a><span data-key="fe9829eac52740459f55b7db7195188c"><span data-offset-key="fe9829eac52740459f55b7db7195188c:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="abac554ade94488d9af83ab8b48d6450"><span data-offset-key="abac554ade94488d9af83ab8b48d6450:0">**27.**</span><span data-offset-key="abac554ade94488d9af83ab8b48d6450:1"> Write a Python program to find the type of the progression (arithmetic progression/geometric progression) and the next successive member of a given three successive members of a sequence. </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/#EDITOR" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="bf8274bfb02a42ce84e62419c48c0cfd"><span data-offset-key="bf8274bfb02a42ce84e62419c48c0cfd:0">Go to the editor</span></span></a><span data-key="ef1917dba7cd4a499720f9f1496fdebf"><span data-offset-key="ef1917dba7cd4a499720f9f1496fdebf:0"> According to Wikipedia, an arithmetic progression (AP) is a sequence of numbers such that the difference of any two successive members of the sequence is a constant. For instance, the sequence 3, 5, 7, 9, 11, 13, . . . is an arithmetic progression with common difference 2. For this problem, we will limit ourselves to arithmetic progression whose common difference is a non-zero integer. On the other hand, a geometric progression (GP) is a sequence of numbers where each term after the first is found by multiplying the previous one by a fixed non-zero number called the common ratio. For example, the sequence 2, 6, 18, 54, . . . is a geometric progression with common ratio 3. For this problem, we will limit ourselves to geometric progression whose common ratio is a non-zero integer. </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/python-basic-1-exercise-27.php" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="c310214ffb1f4b4e85ab8a6c31f9f6f2"><span data-offset-key="c310214ffb1f4b4e85ab8a6c31f9f6f2:0">Click me to see the sample solution</span></span></a><span data-key="e48a1e27ad434742a35a37fc741cc1f9"><span data-offset-key="e48a1e27ad434742a35a37fc741cc1f9:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1c660f399fed456e9c3e3b7e49126971"><span data-offset-key="1c660f399fed456e9c3e3b7e49126971:0">**49.**</span><span data-offset-key="1c660f399fed456e9c3e3b7e49126971:1"> Write a Python program which reads the two adjoined sides and the diagonal of a parallelogram and check whether the parallelogram is a rectangle or a rhombus. </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/#EDITOR" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="82c271d328894acd98d1e2e7e25a8c1f"><span data-offset-key="82c271d328894acd98d1e2e7e25a8c1f:0">Go to the editor</span></span></a><span data-key="9aa84806d0df4c189b54150756a3dfcd"><span data-offset-key="9aa84806d0df4c189b54150756a3dfcd:0"> According to Wikipedia- parallelograms: In Euclidean geometry, a parallelogram is a simple (non-self-intersecting) quadrilateral with two pairs of parallel sides. The opposite or facing sides of a parallelogram are of equal length and the opposite angles of a parallelogram are of equal measure. rectangles: In Euclidean plane geometry, a rectangle is a quadrilateral with four right angles. It can also be defined as an equiangular quadrilateral, since equiangular means that all of its angles are equal (360°/4 = 90°). It can also be defined as a parallelogram containing a right angle. rhombus: In plane Euclidean geometry, a rhombus (plural rhombi or rhombuses) is a simple (non-self-intersecting) quadrilateral whose four sides all have the same length. Another name is equilateral quadrilateral, since equilateral means that all of its sides are equal in length. The rhombus is often called a diamond, after the diamonds suit in playing cards which resembles the projection of an octahedral diamond, or a lozenge, though the former sometimes refers specifically to a rhombus with a 60° angle, and the latter sometimes refers specifically to a rhombus with a 45° angle. The expression of a given even number as a sum of two primes is called a Goldbach partition of that number. The following are examples of Goldbach partitions for some even numbers: 6 = 3 + 3 8 = 3 + 5 10 = 3 + 7 = 5 + 5 12 = 7 + 5 ... 100 = 3 + 97 = 11 + 89 = 17 + 83 = 29 + 71 = 41 + 59 = 47 + 53 Input an even number (0 to exit): 100 Number of combinations: 6 </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/python-basic-1-exercise-53.php" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="29886eca121f46b7a1f1e1146a90e42b"><span data-offset-key="29886eca121f46b7a1f1e1146a90e42b:0">Click me to see the sample solution</span></span></a><span data-key="68270fa382434d72a9861835430cbd6e"><span data-offset-key="68270fa382434d72a9861835430cbd6e:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="bb5228c97dbd4fbda71cc944a3a85325"><span data-offset-key="bb5228c97dbd4fbda71cc944a3a85325:0">**65.**</span><span data-offset-key="bb5228c97dbd4fbda71cc944a3a85325:1"> In mathematics, a subsequence is a sequence that can be derived from another sequence by deleting some or no elements without changing the order of the remaining elements. For example, the sequence (A,B,D) is a subsequence of (A,B,C,D,E,F) obtained after removal of elements C, E, and F. The relation of one sequence being the subsequence of another is a preorder. The subsequence should not be confused with substring (A,B,C,D) which can be derived from the above string (A,B,C,D,E,F) by deleting substring (E,F). The substring is a refinement of the subsequence. The list of all subsequences for the word "apple" would be "a", "ap", "al", "ae", "app", "apl", "ape", "ale", "appl", "appe", "aple", "apple", "p", "pp", "pl", "pe", "ppl", "ppe", "ple", "pple", "l", "le", "e", "". <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="823b0ecd762f4610be30b6a03b4d451a"><span data-offset-key="823b0ecd762f4610be30b6a03b4d451a:0">**66.**</span><span data-offset-key="823b0ecd762f4610be30b6a03b4d451a:1"> From Wikipedia, the free encyclopaedia: A happy number is defined by the following process: Starting with any positive integer, replace the number by the sum of the squares of its digits, and repeat the process until the number equals 1 (where it will stay), or it loops endlessly in a cycle which does not include 1. Those numbers for which this process ends in 1 are happy numbers, while those that do not end in 1 are unhappy numbers. <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="28b891e088764564956c5a8f956f2a6e"><span data-offset-key="28b891e088764564956c5a8f956f2a6e:0">**67.**</span><span data-offset-key="28b891e088764564956c5a8f956f2a6e:1"> From Wikipedia, A happy number is defined by the following process: Starting with any positive integer, replace the number by the sum of the squares of its digits, and repeat the process until the number equals 1 (where it will stay), or it loops endlessly in a cycle which does not include 1. <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="b4cfbf56fc6f4db99ad9902cc9ceb900"><span data-offset-key="b4cfbf56fc6f4db99ad9902cc9ceb900:0">If you are a reader of Greek mythology, then you are probably familiar with Narcissus. He was a hunter of exceptional beauty that he died because he was unable to leave a pool after falling in love with his own reflection. That's why I keep myself away from pools these days (kidding). In mathematics, he has kins by the name of narcissistic numbers - numbers that can't get enough of themselves. In particular, they are numbers that are the sum of their digits when raised to the power of the number of digits. For example, 371 is a narcissistic number; it has three digits, and if we cube each digits 33 + 73 + 13 the sum is 371. Other 3-digit narcissistic numbers are 153 = 13 + 53 + 33 370 = 33 + 73 + 03 407 = 43 + 03 + 73. There are also 4-digit narcissistic numbers, some of which are 1634, 8208, 9474 since 1634 = 14+64+34+44 8208 = 84+24+04+84 9474 = 94+44+74+44 It has been proven that there are only 88 narcissistic numbers (in the decimal system) and that the largest of which is 115,132,219,018,763,992,565,095,597,973,971,522,401 has 39 digits.Ref.: //</span></span><a href="https://bit.ly/2qNYxo2" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="f1168cace0d046178dd8bf6dba2558f8"><span data-offset-key="f1168cace0d046178dd8bf6dba2558f8:0">https://bit.ly/2qNYxo2</span></span></a><span data-key="a0004b86796b455e8b26d7440961cdb0"><span data-offset-key="a0004b86796b455e8b26d7440961cdb0:0"> Sample Input: (153) (370) (407) (409) (1634) (8208) (9474) (9475)</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="99622b22ed76496a9202ce9ce3667157"><span data-offset-key="99622b22ed76496a9202ce9ce3667157:0">**105.**</span><span data-offset-key="99622b22ed76496a9202ce9ce3667157:1"> Write a Python program to check whether a given sequence is linear, quadratic or cubic. </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/#EDITOR" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="2302f18b9fc248d7b79709f49fd9db6c"><span data-offset-key="2302f18b9fc248d7b79709f49fd9db6c:0">Go to the editor</span></span></a><span data-key="3e4c7c5dd6044613aa6933be7ccd8ee7"><span data-offset-key="3e4c7c5dd6044613aa6933be7ccd8ee7:0"> Sequences are sets of numbers that are connected in some way. Linear sequence: A number pattern which increases or decreases by the same amount each time is called a linear sequence. The amount it increases or decreases by is known as the common difference. Quadratic sequence: In quadratic sequence, the difference between each term increases, or decreases, at a constant rate. Cubic sequence: Sequences where the 3rd difference are known as cubic sequence. Sample Input: \[0,2,4,6,8,10\] \[1,4,9,16,25\] \[0,12,10,0,-12,-20\] \[1,2,3,4,5\] Sample Output: Linear Sequence Quadratic Sequence Cubic Sequence Linear Sequence </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/python-basic-1-exercise-105.php" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="c4f2e904984549598d17c884748170df"><span data-offset-key="c4f2e904984549598d17c884748170df:0">Click me to see the sample solution</span></span></a><span data-key="02aa9c5e56d64af4a4315d94f63d9d36"><span data-offset-key="02aa9c5e56d64af4a4315d94f63d9d36:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span></span>

Since division of integers by zero is undefined, this definition has meaning only if a and b are both different from zero. However, some authors define lcm(a,0) as 0 for all a, which is the result of taking the lcm to be the least upper bound in the lattice of divisibility. <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0a47c643230e412d9964e24aa3b20295"><span data-offset-key="0a47c643230e412d9964e24aa3b20295:0">**141.**</span><span data-offset-key="0a47c643230e412d9964e24aa3b20295:1"> Write a Python program to get the domain name using PTR DNS records from a given IP address. </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/#EDITOR" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="e7dd5fe351d1445d8835b100603c94bc"><span data-offset-key="e7dd5fe351d1445d8835b100603c94bc:0">Go to the editor</span></span></a><span data-key="796f056600d943f18f71620ba124a60b"><span data-offset-key="796f056600d943f18f71620ba124a60b:0"> Domain name using PTR DNS: dns.google ec2-13-251-106-90.ap-southeast-1.compute.amazonaws.com dns.google ec2-23-23-212-126.compute-1.amazonaws.com </span></span><a href="https://www.w3resource.com/python-exercises/basic/python-basic-1-exercise-141.php" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="0210e81298924bbcb771436b135b6f30"><span data-offset-key="0210e81298924bbcb771436b135b6f30:0">Click me to see the sample solution</span></span></a><span data-key="af3a4a6197bc4db3b5eae42e3f0e8b75"><span data-offset-key="af3a4a6197bc4db3b5eae42e3f0e8b75:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d2180ad844aa460f86359c6808d80284"><span data-offset-key="d2180ad844aa460f86359c6808d80284:0">**142.**</span><span data-offset-key="d2180ad844aa460f86359c6808d80284:1"> Write a Python program to check if every consecutive sequence of zeroes is followed by a consecutive sequence of ones of same length in a given string.
